B000002GC2|5.0|I'm sure there are a lot of people out there who would look back on this album who'd feel something of a guilt complex for liking an album whose musical virtues lay so heavily in it's production. Sad to say even....a member of my family who I won't name out of respect didn't find this album particularly to their liking for a long time. Perhaps one of the reasons why this album has such a different reputation is that it represents a somewhat different Quincy Jones than the one who recorded [[ASIN:B000002GC4 Sounds & Stuff Like That]] several years earlier. That was a recording that owed a great deal of it's musical energy to funk and R&B. This on the other hand showcased a lot more sleek urban contemporary pop considerations and in the end sounds perhaps like a slightly more commercial record that Quincy had done up to this point. This was released around the time of Mike's [[ASIN:B00005QGAT Off the Wall]],Johnson's [[ASIN:B000002GF0 Light Up the Night]] and Patti Austin's [[ASIN:B003UI50CM Every Home Should Have One]]. If your already fully familiar with those three albums, than it may not be too necessary for you to read this review since you already sort of know the sound of it. For those who aren't,here's what this is.<br /><br />  One of the singer/songwriter/instrumentalists who caught Quincy's ear around this time was Englands Chas Jankel from Ian Dury's Blockheads. His debut [[ASIN:B000A7BBHY Chas Jankel]] contained a song called \\"Ai No Corrida\\" that is presented here and,while not as musically abstract it is more \\"Quincified\\" along with the lead vocals of Charles May who takes  the very British vocal affectations of Chas away from this version. The title song is the most thoroughly funk oriented number here with a stomping beat,bassline and keyboard solo with Q himself rapping as \\"the dude\\" along with an artist Quincy was at the time just in the process of developing-James Ingram. The multi talented singer/musician/songwriter is featured on the slower ballads here such as \\"Just Once\\" and \\"One Hundred Ways\\",which without surprise became the biggest hits. But they're actually far from the best songs. Those were sung by Patti Austin. \\"Betcha Wouldn't Hurt Me\\" and the compulsive \\"Razzanatazz\\" are both key uptempo songs here with a vibrant sophistifunk sound to them. \\"Somethin' Special\\" is a similar but slower sort of song,maybe even a little more jazzy and another highlight with a rather adult romance audience in mind. \\"Velas\\" is a similarly themed instrumental featuring one of Q's old hands Toots Theilmans on harmonica and whistling. There were stories I heard that the closing dance funk of \\"Turn On The Action\\" was originally intended for Michael Jackson. And honestly it does rather sound like him.<br /><br />  Believe it or not,a persons appreciation of this album will probably have to stem from whether they really take the urban contemporary jazz-sophistifunk sound of the early 80's very seriously. Coming during the first years of the post disco era,where music of a certain rhythmic and racial signitures couldn't be played on a lot of radio stations this along with similarly style albums during this time by people like Grover Washington Jr,Al Jarreau and Michael Franks also receive a similar treatment as being at best \\"a guilty pleasure\\" and as worst \\"a sell out\\". Honestly I have absolutely no guilty feelings whatsoever in the level of enjoyment I have for this album. If your a fan of edgy,angst ridden albums filled with a \\"keeping it real\\" attitude with some variant of hip-hop or neo soul rhythmic pattern to it...no I'd have to agree this album probably isn't going to be your cup of tea. Whenever you have the level of production,musicianship and songwriting/vocal power you have with something like this it's from a time and a place where the musical approach was very different. And above all being done by a whole other generation as well. No putting down anyone. But there's just a different spirit behind this than muchof...well even what Quincy himself is currently doing musically. B0053Q9DHW|5.0|If you are not familiar with, or a fan of, the classic novel by Charlotte Bronte, you may not \\"get\\" the significance of the story, especially with Cary Fukinangas extremely stark retelling; he uses silence and visuals more than dialouge. I first read the book when I was 10 years old; then, a man like Rochester seemed a romantic ideal. Of course, a few decades later, and some experience with the male of the species under my belt, I have a different take on the whole scenario ;)<br /><br />For a young man, director Cary Fukinaga captures the true character of Rochester. Or perhaps precisely because he is male, he nailed the exact nature of the beast when he read the book; women tend to want to see the best in men they are attracted to, make excuses, and think the power of their kiss can turn a frog into a prince. This film makes very little excuse for Rochester. One must remember, although the Bronte sisters are the famous ones, they grew up very close to their only brother, a young man who drank, gambled and womanized to excess, and was undoubtedly mentally ill. In the parlance of the times, he became \\"disipated\\", eventually dying young in a \\"fit of madness\\", leaving his family with a lot of guilt and unanswered questions about what went wrong. I think in some fashion, Charlotte, who herself had very little experience with men outside her dysfunctional family, tried to save and redeem this brother in her romantic portrayal of Rochester. This film strips off the romance, and leaves nothing but stark reality.<br /><br />Mia Waskowski made an excellent Jane, her nonverbal communication is excellent, but a little more dialouge would have been nice; if you have not read the book, you may wonder what makes Jane so special, and why she is crazy for Rochester in the first place. In the book, the intellectual banter between the two sealed the deal, but here, you are left wondering, other than the fact that Jane has very few options in regards to \\"dating\\", why she would fall so hard for a selfish, secretive, cynical man like Rochester. I also must commend Judy Densch on her portrayal of Mrs. Fairfax; protective of Jane, with a wisdom she really can't express. She's a woman who has had to make the best of her \\"place\\" in life.<br /><br />Cary Fukanaga also properly captured the occult and metaphysical overtones of the book; the \\"demon in the attic\\", as metaphor for what haunts Rochester. Married off for money, Rochester's wife turned out to be a mentally ill woman of low moral character (oddly, just like Charlotte Bronte's real life brother) But, rather than sending her away to a mental institution, he secretly locks her away in the attic of his house. The scene in the attic was quite telling; Rochester tenderly embraces the \\"succubus\\", exclaiming, \\"my old demon\\", and allows her to triumphantly spit on the virginal Jane. In another scene, Rochester takes hold of Jane, proclaiming he wants what she has locked safely inside herself; her Soul. Why? Because Jane's unsullied Soul would \\"regenerate him with a vengence\\". Is Rochester really capable of Love? Or has he himself become a sort of \\"incubus\\", a \\"demon\\" in search of a Soul? 0061353248|5.0|For me, this book was terribly fascinating. Dan Ariely does such an excellent job explicating all the ways that our thinking falls apart at the economic level. He exposes all the weaknesses of our psychology. There is a silver lining, however, in that being aware of these weaknesses will help us because the conditions that bring them about are predictable. Hence, \\"predictably irrational.\\"  Ariely's writing style is lucid and frequently humorous. He's probably one of the few authors I have read that have told me in such detail why I'm stupid and make me laugh about that fact. There's so many lessons in here on how to avoid the failings that I'm going to have a hard time keeping track of them all. Nonetheless, I feel that by merely reading this I'm walking away a bit wiser in my spending habits and the marketing that I encounter on a daily basis.  I would be interested in knowing if there's anybody out there trying to counter Ariely's work. I know that he is a behavioral economist which somewhat seeks to undermine classic economics (particularly the assumption that we make rational, economic decisions). If anybody knows of a work contra Ariely, I would be interested in hearing about it. Likewise, if anybody knows of any other books that are along these lines (except for The Upside of Irrationality, which I have pending in my to-read pile), I would like to hear about those as well.  The book has been of practical use to me as well. I have found myself in predicaments similar to those that Ariely discusses and, recognizing them, switched to a more \\"aware\\" contemplation of what was going on. So it's true (for me, at least) that being aware of our own faults will help us catch ourselves from falling into them.

Or to say this another way the American method of indulging the children of doing everything to make them happy of instantly gratifying their every need proves to be a loser in the long run. It is damaging ultimately to the children. The message of the book is that if you enable the children through hard work to develop real ability and skill they will have the kind of success and self- respect in life which will be good to them. The way to be good is to be hard on them when they are young so that they can reap the rewards of their success in life.<br />The book that tells the story of this ethic in practice is written by the mother Amy Chua. She reveals herself to be a particularly difficult and extreme practicioner of what she calls the 'Chinese method of education' Her daughter Sophia is the pianist, her second daughter Lulu is the violinist and Amy is at every lesson and practice, pushing the children on. She gives them total attention and dedication works hard for them. And when accused of doing it all for herself she says that the Chinese view is that the children are extensions of one's own self. She was the perfectly obedient daughter to her own highly successful father and to her mother and this is what she expects from her children.<br />She herself has 'global reputation' and is a successful law- professor as is her husband. She thus does not speak to much about the Jewish success ethic ,if there is such a thing. I found this book by reading through the reviews and most of them suggested that this book was promising. Unfortunately this was not my experience:  First of all, the book has been translated and reads like the chapters were either written for seperate publication or that a long time had passed before the next was started. The descriptions of the NDE's are the best part. Stories of people's life changing experiences often told with their own words are inherently gripping and the promise of what the book's subject implies about our own future is ultimately more important. But this book piggybacks on those things without adding anything more compelling.

B005J29HCQ|5.0|Note:  This review also applies to [[ASIN:B004RFYEEC The Smile Sessions Box Set]].<br /><br />Other than The Beatles' GET BACK album, which still has not been released in its original form (the Spectorized [[ASIN:B0025KVLV0 Let It Be (Remastered)]] and the remixed, de-Spectorized [[ASIN:B0000DJZA5 Let It Be... Naked]] notwithstanding), The Beach Boys' SMiLE project is the most famous (and maybe infamous) unreleased album in rock history.  Originally planned as a follow-up to 1966's [[ASIN:B00005ASHM Pet Sounds]], Brian Wilson scrapped the project in mid-1967 after months of work, believing he had lost his competition with The Beatles, and the pressures from the other Beach Boys, plus legal problems with Capitol Records, finally wore him down.<br /><br />A replacement album, [[ASIN:B000002TR9 Smiley Smile]], cobbled together by the group using only the \\"Good Vibrations\\" single and fragments from the original sessions - the rest of the album was rerecorded - was a critical and commercial flop.  Fragments of SMiLE were issued on later Beach Boys albums such as [[ASIN:B000002TRD 20/20]] and [[ASIN:B000008DA7 Surf's Up]].  In 1993, about an hour of lost SMiLE music was issued on the [[ASIN:B000002UR4 Good Vibrations: Thirty Years Of The Beach Boys]] boxed set.  Then, in 2004, Brian Wilson and Van Dyke Parks completed a new version of SMiLE and recorded it with Brian's new band, The Wondermints ([[ASIN:B0002LI11M Brian Wilson Presents Smile]]); a live concert version, recorded earlier that year in London, was also issued on DVD.<br /><br />Now, with the impending 50th anniversary of The Beach Boys approaching, Capitol Records, along with Brian, has released two versions of THE SMiLE SESSIONS - a basic two-disc version, and a nine-disc box set (five CDs, two vinyl LPs, and two vinyl 45 RPM singles).  The first CD, which contains the same contents in both releases, contains a newly revised SMiLE album, using the same running order as the 2004 remake, giving us an idea of what the album would have sounded like in 1967.  Most of the tracks are mono, as Brian always preferred; he was deaf in one ear, and could not hear stereo sound properly, and as a producer, he believed that only mono mixes could present the music to the listener as he wanted it heard.  Stereo, Brian believed, left too much to the listener's equipment setup.  While I would have loved to have a stereo version of the album, as was done with [[ASIN:B000002U4U The Pet Sounds Sessions]] box set in 1996, the producers explained that unlike Pet Sounds, most of the multi-track masters and many of the components were lost, so a stereo remix of SMiLE was impossible to produce.<br /><br />The second CD of the first version contains session highlights from \\"Our Prayer\\" to \\"Good Vibrations\\" - more than enough to satisfy the casual Beach Boy fan.  The deluxe box set is aimed more at collectors and die-hards, and what a collection it is.  CD1 is identical to the first version, but CDs 2 through 5 contain a very comprehensive view of the SMiLE sessions.  So comprehensive, in fact, that the \\"Heroes and Villains\\" sections take up about 90 percent of CD2, and the \\"Good Vibrations\\" sessions take up all of CD5.  While somewhat repetitive, the session tapes offer fascinating listening, showing Brian's perfectionism and dedication to getting the right sound.  I'm sure he drove the other musicians and the other Beach Boys crazy, but it was obviously worth the effort.<br /><br />The two-LP vinyl album in the deluxe edition follows tracks 1-19 of CD1 for the first three sides.  The fourth side contains rare stereo mixes that are not included on the CDs.  The two 45s are the singles that never were, the two-part \\"Heroes and Villains\\" single, and the \\"Vega-Tables\\"/\\"Surf's Up\\" single.<br /><br />Packaging and amenities are impressive.  The two-disc set includes a colorful 36-page booklet, a SMiLE button, and a fold-out poster of the album artwork.  The deluxe edition is even more impressive; the artwork on the box cover has 3-D graphics; the inside of the box lid has the original back cover of the Duophonic (fake stereo) release of the album, had it been issued.  Inside the box are a 60-page hardcover book with additional essays and a complete sessionography; a double-gatefold sleeve with slots for all five CDs and the two vinyl 45s; the two-record vinyl album in a mono jacket with a gatefold sleeve and a 10\\" photo album inside; and a giant-economy-size version of the album artwork poster.<br /><br />The casual fan will probably make do with the two-disc set, but collectors will want both.<br /><br />I'd love to see The Beatles and Apple do a similar box set for the GET BACK SESSIONS, not to mention the long-lost LET IT BE DVD.<br /><br />Some additional observations:<br /><br />1) The vinyl LP and singles sound fine. I especially enjoyed the stereo mixes on Side 4, but wish that they had been on the CD releases. Although I grew up with vinyl, after listening to CDs for over 20 years, vinyl just sounds flat.<br /><br />2) The 45 versions of \\"Vega-Tables\\" and \\"Surf's Up\\" are the same as on the LP and CD.<br /><br />3) If you have the big box set, \\"Heroes and Villains Part 1\\" and \\"Heroes and Villains Part 2\\" are only available on the vinyl 45, though the individual modules for these tracks are probably scattered throughout the four Sessions CDs. The only way to get the full versions of \\"Heroes and Villains Part 1\\" and \\"Heroes and Villains Part 2\\" on CD is to buy the two-disc set (Tracks 2 and 3 on CD2). All of the other tracks on that disc can be found on the session box set, although some of them are edited (particularly the \\"Good Vibrations\\" sessions).<br /><br />4) I compared the 20/20 versions of \\"Cabin Essence\\" and \\"Our Prayer,\\" and the 1971 version of \\"Surf's Up,\\" to the SMiLE versions. Save for stereo remixing and overdubs, the versions are almost identical. It's amazing that the 1968 overdubbed vocals on \\"Our Prayer\\" are almost perfectly in sync with the 1966 originals - another tribute to Brian's production genius.box lid has the original back cover of the Duophonic (fake stereo) release of the album, had it been issued.  Inside the box are a 60-page hardcover book with additional essays and a complete sessionography; a double-gatefold sleeve with slots for all five CDs and the two vinyl 45s; the two-record vinyl album in a mono jacket with a gatefold sleeve and a 10\\" photo album inside; and a giant-economy-size version of the album artwork poster.  The casual fan will probably make do with the two-disc set, but collectors will want both.  I'd love to see The Beatles and Apple do a similar box set for the GET BACK SESSIONS, not to mention the long-lost LET IT BE DVD. 0679731369|5.0|--I cant imagine any serious writer *not* wolfing this fare down as greedily as I did. It is--at its heart--primarily a story about telling stories. But that isnt to say that a non-writer wont enjoy \\"Flaubert's Parrot.\\" I can hardly imagine any serious reader not being fascinated, as I was, by what Barnes has to say and the elegant, inventive, often unpredictable way he says it.<br /><br />\\"Flaubert's Parrot\\" is part literary criticism, part biography, part detective story, part love story. And if that's not enough, its also a cosmic musing on the nature of life and death, truth and beauty, Art, deception and survival. The novel's protagonist, a retired doctor turned amateur Flaubert scholar, is a widower coming to terms with the loose ends that remain at the end of a non-fictional life, not only his wife's, not only Flaubert's, but his own. A book, to paraphrase Barnes, is where everything is explained to you in the end. Life, on the other hand, is where nothing is explained.<br /><br />In tracking down the \\"facts\\" of Flaubert's life, including what became of the stuffed parrot Flaubert used as inspiration for one of his stories, Geoffrey Braithwaite, the semi-reliable narrator of \\"Flaubert's Parrot,\\" defends, admires, and sympathizes with his subject warts and all, even as he acknowledges that the \\"truth\\" of Flaubert--or any of us-- is a horizon that stubbornly and forever recedes no matter how diligently and relentlessly we march towards it.<br /><br />Barnes is a beautiful prose stylist. His knowledge is encyclopedic. His narrative is full of trivia, gossip, historical sidebars, and digressions as fascinating as the main thrust of the plot. And there most definitely is a plot, in the conventional sense; but it's delivered by means of various inventive plot devices that keep a reader on his or her toes, forcing us to see anew the object of his obsession (Flaubert, ostensibly, but so much more than the dead literary genius) from so many different perspectives we are left dazzled and speechless by the awesome complexity of it all. Barnes will not be appreciated for these qualities, the very qualities that make him such a superb writer, by a good many readers, those who prefer their narratives served up ABCDE with an explanatory epilogue as dessert. Nor will he be appreciated by readers who don't understand why anyone should care about Literature. Or Flaubert. Or his parrot.<br /><br />Dont let the reviews of such readers dissuade you.<br /><br />This is a 5-star book if there ever was one. The first Julian Barnes book I ever read; it won't be my last (unless I drop dead before tomorrow when I plan to start another... \\"The History of the World in 10 1/2 Chapters.\\"s by the awesome complexity of it all. 1455510319|5.0|As the other reviewers have noted, this is the fourth novel by Michael Connelly featuring Mickey Haller, the Lincoln Lawyer.  This novel focuses on a criminal defense trial for a Haller client who is accused of murdering a bank employee.  The background story is the sordid and messy real estate foreclosure world of the last five years.  Haller begins representing the client when the foreclosure case is filed.  Later, when the murder occurs, he is her criminal defense lawyer.  The major point about this book is that Connelly really understands the criminal defense process.  The trial scenes make sense and you get the feeling of how the criminal system works, and sometimes goes awry.    Haller sounds and feels like a lawyer - he acts like a real lawyer and he tries hard for his clients, even when they may be guilty.  You could not have a better portrayal of a criminal defense lawyer and the crazy California justice system.  Lots of people try to write about the legal system - it is very difficult to make it feel right.  Connelly, who is not a lawyer, succeeds admirably.
0062011847|4.0|Among Beethoven aficionados there are two camps.  The first group are those who view and evaluate Beethoven's later works in the context of his deafness, for which they are easily considered feats of genius. The second group, an uncompromising bunch, considers them on an absolute basis. They view them as more flawed and occasionally discordant than his earlier works (which by the way, were not written by a deaf guy.)   I am in the first camp -- I have far too much love and respect for Pratchett and his Discworld not to acknowledge the truth about the author -- that he is unable to use the primary tool of his craft (ie. the keyboard) and is now forced to dictate his stories into a machine.  Snuff feels dictated, as simple as that.  Anyone who has ever tried to dictate knows that it is devilishly hard: sentences tend to run-on, and ideas tend to repeat themselves. These flaws are certainly evident in Snuff.  Those who argue here that Snuff feels \\"ghost written\\" are, in my opinion, off the mark. A ghost writer takes a series of facts and simplifies -- by constructing a single voice and a plausible narrative. Ghost written books, above all, feel consistent.  Snuff doesn't feel ghost written; and in fact, to me it doesn't feel written at all.  It feels spoken, authentically and inconsistently in Pratchett's own voice, and then edited and compiled with a (too) light and respectful hand.   It is amazing to me that anyone can write a novel under these circumstances, just as it is astonishing to me that deaf Beethoven could compose symphonies that sounded, well, symphonic.  If I were to rate SNUFF on the basis of a novel dictated by a person suffering from Alzheimer's, I'd not only rate it five stars, I'd rate it a full fledged act of genius.    Standing on its own, if you are the kind of reader who welcomes a return to Discworld and its characters-- with a predictable but serviceable plot that still moves along...then Snuff is a pleasing, 3-4 star holiday vacation with a few noticeable flaws.

0060763280|5.0|Rather than review this book from scratch, I'm going to address two criticisms of it that are leveled at it commonly enough by other reviewers to conceivably dissuade potential readers from buying it.<br /><br />CRITICISM #1: LITTLE IN THE BOOK IS ORIGINAL<br /><br />This is true. But so what? This is the case for some of the best books out there. Why? Because there is a finite number of tried-and-true insights, perspectives, and techniques one can employ and adopt to improve one's life and profitability. Does this mean no one should bother writing books anymore? No. Why? Because what distinguishes one author from another in the success genre is not necessarily how unique their ideas are, but more often how well they manage to combine ideas borrowed from others and internalized well enough to lend themselves to their own distinct recipe for success conveyed through their books. Eker does a good job in this area. While he may not give enough credit to the giants in the field upon whose shoulders he stands, this doesn't reduce the impact these ideas have for the reader open to being educated and inspired by them, irrespective of who gets the credit for the ideas.<br /><br />CRITICISM #2: EKER SHAMELESSLY PROMOTES HIS SEMINARS THROUGHOUT THE BOOK<br /><br />This is both true and false. It's true that he promotes his seminars, but it's important to note that he only starts doing so most conspicuously and, one must admit, somewhat annoyingly in the later parts of his book, after one has already read what I consider to be the most profound, helpful sections of the book anyway. By that time we have already benefited enough from what Eker has written to induce us to give him some slack, to acknowledge that, while his constant reference to his seminars later in the book is somewhat shameless, one is more inclined to forgive him for his effrontery; at least he proves himself before doing so, leaving it to the reader to decide whether or not to attend his seminars. It's worth noting, however, that in these later portions of the book, not only does the seminar marketing begin to be piled on thick, but--and perhaps most objectionably--it's obvious that the material surrounding these pitches is secondary in importance for Eker than the pitches themselves.<br /><br />So, is the book worth buying? I think so. It contains several worthwhile insights with the potential to inspire one to achieve more than one might achieve without the benefit of this book and others like it. And so what if there are others like it? What matters is that whatever potentially life-changing ideas it contains are acknowledged, digested, and utilized by its readers, regardless of who might have come up with the ideas first. For example, when Eker emphasizes, at the beginning of the book, the importance of developing a &#34;money blueprint&#34; that promotes success, and more generally the fact that one's ability to succeed outwardly is a direct result of a correspondingly success-oriented inner relationship to money and what it represents, he echoes most if not all of the success authors who preceded him. Must one buy Eker's book as well? Perhaps &#34;must&#34; is too strong a word. Does it hurt? Certainly not. The key is to reinforce powerful ideas, and repetition is the most obvious way of doing so. After all, one of the qualities Eker champions in his book is the &#34;modeling&#34; of the worthy ideas of others. To chastise him for practicing what he preaches in this regard would be rather foolish, and, depending on the (in this case rather formidable) quality of what happens to be modeled, possibly detrimental to one's chances of ultimately succeeding.tions of the book, not only does the seminar marketing begin to be piled on thick, but--and perhaps most objectionably--it's obvious that the material surrounding these pitches is secondary in importance for Eker than the pitches themselves.  So, is the book worth buying? I think so. It contains several worthwhile insights with the potential to inspire one to achieve more than one might achieve without the benefit of this book and others like it. And so what if there are others like it? What matters is that whatever potentially life-changing ideas it contains are acknowledged, digested, and utilized by its readers, regardless of who might have come up with the ideas first. For example, when Eker emphasizes, at the beginning of the book, the importance of developing a &#34;money blueprint&#34; that promotes success, and more generally the fact that one's ability to succeed outwardly is a direct result of a correspondingly success-oriented inner relationship to money and what it represents, he echoes most if not all of the success authors who preceded him. 0307913147|5.0|Ready Player One, Ernest Cline's 2011 debut novel, is set in the grim future of 2044, where economic collapse and energy shortages make life unbearable. Or, at least, part of it is. In this dystopian future, most people spend their days connected to the OASIS, an online virtual world where people can forget their troubles and be whoever they want to be while exploring thousands of exotic planets. Several years before the story begins, James Halliday, the eccentric creator of the OASIS who was obsessed with the 1980's of his youth, dies, leaving behind a message that the first person to solve his cryptic riddles and find a hidden easter egg he's left in the OASIS would win his huge personal fortune and control of Halliday's company, Gregarious Simulation Systems.<br /><br />Our hero, Wade Watts - or, as he's known in the OASIS, Parzival (Percival was taken) - is an 18 year old who has only known misery in the real world, but has spent five years hunting Halliday's easter egg and mastering every bit of obscure '80's trivia he can find to try to solve Halliday's obscure clues. Parzival's friends Aech (mentor and best buddy), Art3mis (rival and love interest), Daito and Shoto (a pair from Japan working as a team) are also gunters (as the egg hunters are known), and become his friendly competition.<br /><br />The unfriendly competition comes from Innovative Online Industries, led by the ruthless Nolan Sorrento. IOI and their hordes of corporate lackeys known as Sixers are also looking for the egg, so that they can rule the OASIS and \\"monetize\\" it properly. Monthly fees, advertising everywhere, an end to the guaranteed anonymity of the OASIS - it would be an end to life as Wade's generation knows it, and IOI will stop at nothing to reach the egg first and make it a reality.<br /><br />The novel is filled to the brim with '80's references from film, television, video games, books, Dungeons & Dragons, and more, and anyone who grew up in that era will appreciate many of them. Cline does a great job of explaining them so that even if you don't have the trivia mastery Parzival does, you can keep up, and he makes it entertaining. I was born in 1978 (or, to put in Ready Player One terms, my first video game system was an Intellivision, but I spent way more time playing on the NES), while the fictional Halliday and the real Cline and Wheaton were all born in 1972, so there are a few references to the really early '80's I wasn't initially familiar with, but it didn't slow me down.<br /><br />As fun as the fantastic voyages into the OASIS are, Cline does a great job of keeping the action in the real world important and exciting. Parzival finding the hidden keys, leveling up, and getting epic gear are very well done and entertaining, but the heart of the book is Wade's story and his personal growth and the struggle to juggle his quest for the egg and the need to survive and make personal connections in the real world. Those were the parts that really made me think (as I sit here typing the review to post in the internet - yes, I'm aware of the irony).<br /><br />Actor, writer, and geek Wil Wheaton narrates the book, and he does a fantastic job. His characterization of Wade/Parzival and his friends and rivals really makes the story come alive. You can tell that Wheaton loves the material as much as Cline and Parzival, and experienced it himself as he perfectly renders all the movie quotes and classic video game sound effects of the era. Truly, I cannot think of another audiobook narrator who could nail the Pac-Man death bwoop like Wil does. This is one of those rare combinations of book and audio narrator that really adds another level of enjoyment to the experience.<br /><br />Ready Player One is one of the best books I've read this year, and it has definitely put Cline on my radar as an author to watch. The film rights have been sold and Cline (who is also a scriptwriter) is working on the script. I'd love to see Ready Player One on the big screen. In the meantime, I heartily recommend you read Ready Player One, and if you are an audio book fan, definitely listen to it and enjoy Wheaton's fantastic performance.<br /><br />The only thing that would have improved the book for me would be if Parzival was a descendent of Erdrick.Player One, and if you are an audio book fan, definitely listen to it and enjoy Wheaton's fantastic performance.  The only thing that would have improved the book for me would be if Parzival was a descendent of Erdrick.

B005DHC6R0|5.0|For Americans who have not been paying attention to Noel Gallagher-related music (Oasis) since the heyday of the 1990s, now is a great time to listen again.  With this album, Noel Gallagher has delivered a heartfelt, well-produced pop-rock album, packed with great melodies and interesting music.  Most of the professional reviews of Noel Gallagher's first solo album spend too much time writing about the interesting backstory (and the whole Liam vs. Noel thing), which I find a bit unfortunate as it overshadows the great music here.    There's a variety of quality songs here, each reflecting Noel's melodic gifts.  A few of the songs go for the epic feel of classic Oasis tunes [Everybody's on the Run,(I Wanna Live in a Dream in My) Record Machine and Stop the Clocks], except with strings and choir instead of electric guitar.  On paper that sounds pretty silly, but it actually works, once you get used to it.  A few other songs have an interesting Kinks influence [The Death of You and Me, Dream On, Soldier Boys and Jesus Freaks]. There's even a good ballad, If I Had a Gun, and a good quasi-dance tune, AKA What a Life!  The music generally sounds something like a cross between The Importance of Being Idle, Falling Down and Part of the Queue from the last couple of Oasis albums.  I am not a big fan of horns, but the modest use of them on this album works nicely.   Noel's voice sounds great through-out.  I've always been partial to singers who actually write their own lyrics as they can often give the songs emotion and meaning that a more technically proficient singer might miss.  The tone of the album is generally uplifting with a large dose of  melancholy, reflecting Noel's vocal and lyrical style.  The lyrics, while sometimes not Noel's strongest point, are generally good here.  Even when the words are somewhat silly (as on Dream On), Noel's delivery, along with the music, make that silliness fun, not cringe-inducing.    Noel has even managed to leave a few of the more interesting songs off of the album - check out Let the Lord Shine a Light on Me, A Simple Game of Genius and Alone on the Rope on YouTube or elsewhere (such as the Deluxe version or Itunes version of the album).  In particular, Alone on the Rope is a classic that should be on the album somewhere.    Some may not be satisfied with this album because it does not \\"rock\\" or they might miss Liam's voice. True, there are only two guitar solos on the album, and the ending of Stop of the Clocks is about the only time the band cuts loose.  And while I like the production, I still enjoy the more stripped down demo version of Record Machine (again check Youtube) with its great baseline and more raw guitar solo.  Still, this album is the most consistent set of Gallagher-penned tunes since Oasis' commercial peak, What's the Story (Morning Glory), though it does not rise to the heights of that album.

0393064476|5.0|This purports to be about the rediscovery of a lost work by Lucretius that helped to open the door to let in the fresh breezes of the Renaissance.  What it is really about is the fact that in the 1st century B.C. thinkers had come close to a real understanding of the way the world works without having to resort to propitiation of unseen deities.  As Flaubert remarked, \\"Just when the pagan gods had ceased to be, and Christianity had not yet arrived, there was a unique moment in history, between Cicero and Marcus Aurelius, when man stood alone.\\"  p. 71. Greenblatt is trying to ask the question of how it came to be through most of human history that \\"the tellers of fables possess[ed] a monopoly on the measure that humans ha[d] invented to express the pleasure and beauty of the world\\"?  p. 200.  The liberating thought of Lucretius is that \\"knowing the way things are\\" as opposed to knowing only the fictions that those in power invented and perpetuated, would actually \\"awaken the deepest wonder.\\"  What took this approach away from us for close to two millennia, as Greenblatt makes clear, was a medieval church kleptocracy that used the fear of an afterlife to enrich itself and the fear of a painful death to keep otherwise thinking minds within its control. Greenblatt's point here is virtually the same as Richard Dawkins' in his latest book, \\"The Magic of Reality\\": it's time for us to grow up, to get back to the Lucretian project of building a just and peaceful world without the interference of ideological fictions, but growing up does not mean we need to lose our sense of wonder.

It's really impossible to calibrate why this mismatched pair continues to connect over the years other than to fulfill the cardboard dimensions of David Nicholls' verbose screenplay, which is in turn based on his [[ASIN:B0036S4CTS 2009 book]].  The lugubrious result makes for particularly disappointing cinema given that the film was directed by Danish filmmaker Lone Scherfig, who helmed Carey Mulligan's star-making turn in the far more satisfying [[ASIN:B002ONC9NC An Education]] (2009).<br /><br />The story begins on the night of July 15, 1988 when recent Edinburgh University graduates Emma Morley and Dexter Mayhew end up spending the night together. She comes from a working-class background and is consequently smart and caustic but insecure as evidenced by the pair of granny glasses she constantly wears. He comes from money and has the cocky manner befitting someone whose shallow aim in life is to become famous since he's already rich. They connect for no apparent reason from the outset and start a friendship that borders on codependency. We see them every year thereafter on the same day as they grow from young adulthood to early middle age. Emma stumbles out of the gate becoming increasingly depressed working at a Mexican restaurant in London. Dexter finds fame as the sleazy host of a VH1-type entertainment-lite program. She eventually finds her footing as a writer, while he goes through a drug-induced tailspin. Their up-and-down lives intertwine at defining moments when something decisive happens to one, and the other has to cope with whatever the new situation is. The film repeats this pattern so often that it becomes redundant and tiresome even when an inevitable plot device occurs late in the story.<br /><br />I can see why an actress as engaging as Anne Hathaway would be drawn to a part like Emma because she gets to play a wide range of shades and emotions in her character's arc, but there are two things that are off-kilter about her performance. First, her pinched English accent sounds entirely too close to her take-off of Mary Poppins from her guest appearance on [[ASIN:B004DH7XI8 Saturday Night Live]] to be believable. Second, her naturally vibrant presence doesn't lend credibility to the early scenes when she appears to be doing a cheeky throwback to the first [[ASIN:B00006L948 Princess Diaries]] movie. She also makes co-star Jim Sturgess (Jude in [[ASIN:B002G1K82Q Across the Universe]]) look all the more pallid as the pitiable Dexter. He seems to be channeling Hugh Grant in order to evoke the charm necessary to attract Emma, but he suffers even more from the story's thumbnail-sketch approach. As Emma's failed love interest Ian, Rafe Spall is convincingly annoying as an aspiring stand-up comic without any talent, while Romola Garai has the thankless role of Dexter's humorless lover-turned-wife. Both appear to be playing stock characters for the sake of the central love story. At least Patricia Clarkson lends her requisite &eacute;lan as Dexter's worldly mother, but her scenes are all too brief. Despite a clever time-shifting scene at Arthur's Seat in Edinburgh, the film left me dry-eyed wanting to see [[ASIN:B000AP04MO Two for the Road]] again.. B003OF3R0S|5.0|I got into Avenged Sevenfold late, I will admit, but I did take the time to really listen to their previous albums and to hear how they have progressed throughout the years. Each new album was a massive step forward in their growth and progression, each one more brilliant than the last by lightyears. Though, to me, even though Waking the Fallen and City of Evil are their two greatest albums to date, Nightmare stands out as something truly and undeniably special, and not just because of the loss of their drummer, Jimmy \\"the Rev\\" Sullivan. It sweeps us up and away into the start of a train ride into sorrow and fear, followed by the chugging Welcome to the Family, the first step into the meat of the album, so to speak. Danger Line is . . just breathtaking. It's a track I have gone back to over and over again, for its building power and ending jolt that simply blew me away. Buried Alive, Natural Born Killer and So Far Away don't even try to let up on the emotional roller coaster started by the opening trio, and we're swept deeper into a growing nightmare (no pun intended) so tremendous we cannot even begin to grasp the whole of its enormity. And then comes God Hates Us. This track is a climax of outrage; an onslaught of screaming, burning fury so white-hot it will rock you to your very core. This track says, plain and simple: do NOT tick off Avenged Sevenfold! Words that should be heeded, after hearing this monster. For a short moment we're given reprieve for the start of Victim, but that is not to last, and we are quickly ripped back into the deepest pools of sorrow and confusion that will envelop you in its jaw-dropping beauty. Tonight The World Dies is a bit of a relaxing number, letting us recover from the endless barrage that drains you of your emotional integrity. After being smashed time and time again into a wall of hurt and anger, this song is a well-needed rest, and is a wonderful number all its own. And then we hear Fiction. This song is absolutely heartbreaking, as if a friend from beyond the grave was calling out to those they left behind, trying to explain why they left, and asking their loved ones not to grieve and to understand. This is probably the most heart wrenching song on the entire album, but it only sets us up for the next, and final track. We are brought into Save Me slowly, but with the feeling of impending doom as the grinding bass pulls us along into the deepest well of turmoil, yet. If Victim had you reeling, then Save Me will obliterate you. This track is an epic, and the perfect closer. Here we seem to have all of the muddled emotion of the entire album compiled into one ultimate final assault of sadness, anger and confusion leading out into purest acceptance of tragedy. This is, in essence, a deconstruction of the album as a whole, with all of the complexity and emotional burden collapsing in on you before resolving itself entirely. Though this is not actually a concept album, it does feel and flow as one, and is one of the most memorable albums I have ever heard for all the reasons listed above. Post-punk New York and spaghetti Westerns appear repeatedly.  A minimalist twanging Western guitar repeatedly suggests Television without offering any of their skill or much of their lyricism.  A sense of edgy, noir confusion, contradictions, and emotional chaos prevail.  She repeatedly swells to the edge of a major anthem, only to retreat.  The tension is compelling, but frustrating.  I feel a big heart in a scary place, navigating her way, with powerful aspirations but self-protective caution, through grit and threat. The strongest tracks are labeled \\"S\\" below.    1. Rider to the sea.  Brief twangs of a Western guitar from a foreboding, noir spaghetti Western spaced out in a hyper-spare setting.  The building chorus in the closing suggests the looming climax of such a movie.  2. No more words (S). Quietly, steadily chugging--1-2-1-2-1-2. \\"Oh my love\\" is chanted over and over by a yearning female voice somehow cut loose.  Behind, the Western twanger, now vaguely Television-like--leads a setting that boils up steadily into rasping rock in the space between verses.  Repeat.  3. Desire (S--best of CD).  Something close to bagpipes, backed by smacking drums and flourishing guitars, frames an urgent, powerful female voice very close to Patti Smith's.  Time is suspended as the verses build tension, which is finally released in a big anthemic classic rock chorus somewhere between Smith and Springsteen.  Somehow big and quietly steady at the same time.  Is it better suited to protective granite security or the flashing thunder of stadium music?  4. Suzanne and I.  Big New York rock, at once grand, noir, emotional, and held close.  Her dark, resonant voice nears being lyrical.  Big beat; spare, wanging Television-like guitar; leaps between big and small.  Hints of k. d. lang.  5. First we kiss.  Odd punky take on a Brazilian-informed nightclub popsong suddenly blooms into a near-perfect k. d. lang sound-alike in the big-boned choruses.  Back and forth, with a crowded, almost cinematic bridge.  6. The devil.  Spare, lyrical guitar opens, then slides into twanging.  Stop.  Her voice slides in along side the guitar in long, twining, gutterally uttered lines.  Lyrical guitar and deep, sliding voice unwind in long, spare lines.  Here and there, they swoon incautiously near the outer envelope of safety in slow, ¬æ time.  They work toward a dramatic, noir finish that could backstop a scene with a dead-empty desert and big sky in a Spaghetti western--\\"The devil! The devil! The devil!\\"  7. Blackout.  Steadily driving anthem in a spare setting that fills a big space.  Bits of disco, U2, classic rock.  Her voice slides seamlessly from intimate to soaring and back over a closely controlled, energetic big New York setting.    8. I'll be your man  S).  Slick, clipped, Drifters-Latin syncopation drives quietly and steadily.  The twanging guitar laces the space above.  She enters with an urgent, small sultry, voice and builds to anthemic fullness--\\"I'll be your man\\"--then collapses.  Repeat.  She draws on voices and styles I know, but cannot place.  9. Morning light.  Opening phrases feel portentous, pointing to a big, swelling pop song in the offing.  But her voice works quietly and urgently in a spare, New York post-punk setting.  Everything is played down, as though all are whispering.  Sounds are dark and ghostly until they finally build briefly to a swell before collapsing.  10. Love won't be leaving.  Chugging beat of a heart beat.  Quiet, before a crashing wave of guitar turbulence.  Then her gray/gritty New York voice slides in.  The potential for an anthem remains unrealized, confined in a sooty, hazy, tight place.  All is edgy, uncertain, noir--pumped, but pinned.  Her voice seems to struggle from her body, finally opening full lyrical power on the choruses.  The choruses light up Western skies with dramatic emphasis; the action on the ground remains deadly.

Warm, mellow, languid vocal pop unfolds, tempered by shiny sampled brass and a pulse sprinkled along like a long, mid-tempo picket fence in keyboards.  4. Goshen.  Warm simple keyboard paired with warmly crooning vocals in a calm comfort song, a warm memory or reverie.  All quiet and meditative.  And then the tacky trumpets.  And earnest snare drums.  Wonderful!  5. Payne's Bay.  Squealing synthesized fiddles--or is it a field organ?  A gently rolling pop song from the early 1960s (like Burt Bacharach?) emerges.  Now the synthesizer is pumping trumpets and other brass in a steady Salvation Army march tempo with metrically splashing cymbals.  The marchers chant \\"Head strong\\" on and on.  6. The rip tide.  Quietly epic portrait of a lonely, windswept beach with cliffs shedding shattered shale close ashore.  In brass and piano.  Then piano+vocal.  Quiet, steady, and reassuring, but laced with reserved melancholy.  Brass returns.  On and on. The song turns slowly on long, long cycles like the tide.  7. Vagabond.  Quietly perky piano, then brass, in light syncopation, brings Herb Alpert to mind.  As the setting cha-chas along, the warm, soothing vocal enters in slow lines.  Brass takes up the calm lines.  Keyboard does a bit of Baroque arpeggio exercise.  The texture builds in layers.    8. The peacock.  Vocal takes center stage with a simple setting of pump organ and brass swathing him.  Do I hear Tim Buckley near by?!  9. Port of call.  Simple strummed guitar.  Add forthright, warm vocal.  As usual, the guitar sets the pulse; the vocal flows slowly, steady, in smooth lines.  Add tightly cycling (seemingly strummed) piano.  And of course brass, but now in an oompahing ¬æ-time counter rhythm, which rolls over into the piano.  Quite a set piece of development.

0385535139|5.0|I'd like to note right off the top that I read and finished The Litigators over the course of 10 hours of my Sunday afternoon and night......and since the advent of Facebook and its myriad of ways of distracting someone, that has not even come close to happening.  Until now.  It's that engaging and entertaining.  Is it John Grisham's best work?  Of course not.  Is it a rehashing of some of his previous plots?  Absolutely.  Does it matter?  Nope.  I'm sure you saw the summary further up the page:  Finley & Figg, Attorneys at Law, and their new associate.  Oscar Finley is a tired ex-cop who's running out the string;  Wally Figg, a recovering alchoholic dreaming of the big score; David Zinc, the Harvard boy completely burned out after five years in a mega-firm, who wanders into the 'boutique firm' of the others after the best and worst day of his life.  The three of them stumble through some street law, and a really bad attempt to become players in the bigger game.  Right now I'm seeing echoes of The Rainmaker, The Partner, The Street Lawyer, The King of Torts........need I go on?  But unlike the negative reviewers on here, it didn't bother me.  Part of the reason you read Grisham now is the familiarity of it all, the technology changes, but not John's sympathy for the 'common' lawyer and his contempt for larger firms that bully his heroes.  David Zinc is the protagonist here, and while I don't think that his breakdown is given enough backstory, who wouldn't crack after working five years of 90 hour weeks?  He's often the voice of reason with his bosses, but he knows enough to know that he doesn't know a whole lot.  Think Rudy Baylor from The Rainmaker, with a better education and a stable love life.  Yes, you read that right, there's very little romance in the book.  David is happily married, and his wife is actually a pleasant character to read about.  The significant others of Finley and Figg aren't, so it's a nice balance.  The antagonist is the drug company that'spart of the major lawsuit in the piece, but even then the CEO, while not a hero, can credibly be seen as just doing his job.  More or less.  In reality the antagonists are Finley and Figg, in a manner.  Figg thinks that he knows more than he does, and Finley works a lot less than  he should.  But we care about David, and as the novel goes on, he finds himself on firming footing as a lawyer.  He wins and he loses, and learns from both.  For all intents and purposes John Grisham has run low on ideas, that can't be disputed by me.  The Theodore Boone series is perhaps a way for him to get a break from the norm, and those are solid novels in their own right.  That said, I don't need every book to break new ground.  As the saying goes, there are only a few original stories out there, everything else is just a variation.  Anyone expecting the great American novel from John is kidding themselves, and he'd be the first to agree with me.  I just want to be entertained sometimes, and he satisfies that plenty.  Five stars.

0307477479|5.0|This is a compilation of intertwined stories, somewhat like the book Olive Kitteredge. A few of the stories are gems all by themselves, with glimpses into the characters' deepest thoughts and worries. The last two chapters felt like a big reward for sticking with the book to the end. I enjoyed most of the stories on their own, but this is a case where the whole is worth more than the sum of its parts. The most fascinating aspect of the book for me was the ability to see characters from different points of view at different points in their lives. For instance, we get to see how a teenager really sees her parents, how a husband and wife see each other, and how many of the characters see themselves. I was able to find a character in each story that I could relate to in some way, based on different stages of my own life.     At the end I had an AHA moment. This is a great example of postmodernism! I remembered struggling with the concept in school, and now I feel like I get it. The story is deconstructed and put back together again; We are shown the world from multiple perspectives; and (especially in chapter 12) the author plays with a new way to tell a story. The last two chapters were the most creative and most thrilling to me. The author's view of the future world is both funny and cynical, with a clever combination of new and old trends. Young people will certainly relate to the ways in which communication technology is used to reach people and promote concerts. There is also a special nod to people who are old enough to remember how the entire marketing world catered to the (now aging) babyboomers from the time they were children. On the surface, this appears to be a book about the music world, but I think people in marketing, PR and communications will find it quite relevant.  I know that some people are annoyed by the non-linear structure of the book, and give up before reaching the end.  I suppose I might have too, if I didn't have a pending book club meeting to push me along. In the end it was well worth it. Tip: If you're taking some time between chapters, I suggest that you use post-it notes or a character list to keep track of the character names and relationships.

B005CMSDLE|5.0|There is so much about `Hanna' that shouldn't work, and yet it does; remarkably so.  The plot could have easily been clich&eacute;d and rather tired, and yet it feels youthful and modern.  The plot twists could have been predictable (and at times they are) and yet they feel necessary to the progression of the film.  There are depictions here that could have been deemed mere gimmick if handled poorly (especially the bookended scenes and specific phrasing) and yet they are dynamic in scope and wholly effective.<br /><br />There is so much `right' about this movie.<br /><br />`Hanna' tells of a young girl of sixteen who has been trained in isolation by her father to be an assassin.  She has trained her whole life to defend herself against the woman responsible for the death of her mother, the woman who would stop at nothing to end her life.  She has trained for the moment she would be ready to confront and kill this adversary.  When the film opens, Hanna is ready.  Once the switch is flipped (quite literally as well as figuratively) there is no turning back, and Hanna soon finds herself being sabotaged by an enemy smarter than she expected.<br /><br />A word of caution, `Hanna' is extremely violent, even for a PG-13.  The violence is also of a more disturbing nature, considering that the main proprietor of this violence is a young teenage girl.  To see Hanna snap a woman's neck and then shoot countless men in a single scene can be a bit much for some to watch.  The violence is never gory (although, the scene with the man hanging from the ceiling is a tad gruesome) but it is gratuitous and so one should be aware before committing to watching the film that it will be intense, and then some.<br /><br />For me, this film is the perfect amalgam of film arc-types.  It is an action film on the surface, a dramatic love story at the core and is beautifully glossed over with art house flare.<br /><br />I want Joe Wright to direct the next James Bond film.  I know that they decided to change directors with every new film because it adds a layer of individualism to each individual piece, but once Sam Mendes is done (another finely underrated director, so I'm pleased) can the reigns be handed over to Wright?  He shows such depth of cinematic viewpoint in `Hanna'.  The way he weaves emotional tones into the action, as well as the way he stages his scenes to play out like a beautiful painting; it's all so mesmerizing.  He captures action like a pro, but he never allows the action to take over the core themes of the film.  The score is to die for and the cinematography is delicious (especially that opening sequence).  Cate Blanchett may be a caricature, but she's an effective one.  Bana is superb (great accent work), I just wish he was used more.  I also loved seeing Tom Hollander (creepy, seductive, interesting) spice up the film, and young Jessica Barden is astonishingly effective as the `friend' Hanna develops in her travels.  She also has deft comedic timing, which is a raw asset she should cultivate.<br /><br />The girl is sharp.<br /><br />But there really is Saoirse Ronan, and no one else.  She is magnificent here.  Not only does she handle the physical aspect of the performance (the accent, the action scenes, the languages, the presence) but she captures the emotional trauma of the character as well.  She portrays such depth in her eyes, but she never betrays her character's innocence.  You never feel as though Hanna knows `too much', for she is always believably na√Øve, but she manages to get a slick handle on her character's more advanced education, the harsher grasp on her `emotional makeup'.  You never doubt her killer instincts, and yet she really wins you over when she begins to understand love and friendship.<br /><br />I can't wait to see this girl's future!<br /><br />So, despite the thematic issues (more from a moral standpoint and an obvious personal reaction) this is a film I highly recommend.  It is styled perfectly, and yetthe style never trumps substance.  This is a film you can drool over on all respects, for it is as visually stimulating as it is emotionally captivating.it is as visually stimulating as it is emotionally captivating.

B000002UZ1|5.0|Fantastic collection, agreed. I just want fans to know that in some cases, what's on this disc is not the best version of the song.  First, perhaps my favourite `live' track of all time is `While My Guitar Gently Weeps' on George's `Live in Japan' (1992) CD-set. It features a more laid-back less-brittle sound than on the White Album, and has George and Eric Clapton `guitar-'duelling' during the extended finale. Much preferable to the original to my ears.  The version of `Don't Let Me Down' on `Let It Be...Naked' blends the two `live' performances of the song on the roof, and is rawer and gutsier than the single b-side version heard here. Actually, I prefer the un-retouched second take of the performance on the roof; in fact it is my favourite Beatles track, studio or live, period, but available only on bootleg unfortunately.  As for `Across the Universe', many fans will prefer the original version produced, as by George Martin to this one, re-produced by Phil Spector.   The `single' version of `Let It Be' presented here is the best rendition, except in one respect. The Harrison guitar solo on the version on `Let t Be...Naked' is perfection. (It's from the movie.) I just used rudimentary music-editing software to remove the merely adequate solo and replace it with the `Naked' solo. Now it's the best `Let It Be' that will ever be heard on this planet.  Same goes for `The Long and Winding Road'. Spector desecrated it. I personally can't stand to listen to his version at all and will never volunatrily listen through it again. The version on `Let It Be....Naked' (from the film, once again) is terrific, except that Paul toys with some of the words during the middle-eight, making that middle eight inferior to the basic track heard on the `Beatles Anthology III'. Solution: Remove the offending middle-eight from the `Naked' version and substitute the `Anthology' version's middle-eight. Sounds like a dream: no Lennon fumbling on bass, a more feistily-sung and correct middle-eight, and a nice childlike keyboard solo from Billy Preston. Finally, a recording worthy of one of Mac's most beautiful songs. It only took a third of a century to come about.

0393064476|5.0|The Swerve's primum movena is the arrival at a monastery in southern Germany in 1417 of Gian Francesco Poggio Bracciolini, a papal secretary and devoted book hunter, laid off due to the arrest and excommunication of John XXIII. On a dusty shelf in the library Bracciolini finds a copy of On the Nature of Things by Lucretius, an avid believer in the school of Epicurus. The epic poem had gone missing for a thousand years. Greenblatt's preface tells us of his own accidental discovery of a copy of Lucretius' poem in a sale bin, which he bought for the Max Ernst cover for ten cents. Bracciolini had the poem copied, and thus the world swerved. Greenblatt read the poem, and thus The Swerve. Greenblatt's book is part quest, history lesson, and a peep at the biting, silly, boring and dangerous life of a secular scribe toiling away in a clerical setting. The Swerve is enthralling. The sinister intrigue of the curia in the 15th century, peppered with the poem's rediscovery and its impact on scientists, clergy and the Declaration of Independence is alive with funny and awful asides. Bruno's list of to-dos on Mercury's checklist is hysterical, but the price Bruno paid for his humor is horrific. Bracciolini is known today for his lovely handwriting, but his contribution to the world of philosophy is perhaps incalculable. At the heart of this book is the wondrous, compelling, and - to organized religion, heretical - philosophy of Epicurus and his poetic devotee Lucretius that fear is the enemy of life

0596805527|3.0|The proper use case for this book is that it be used as a comprehensive reference material.  I do not recommend that you use this book to learn JavaScript, especially if you're new to programming altogether.  As others have noted, it is highly detailed and includes every single caveat and nuance including browser bugs and workarounds.  The fact that David Flanagan is an authority on JavaScript and that this book is *the* definitive guide is not up for debate, IMO.  However, this material - its level of detail and its organization - is not conducive to learning, so I do not recommend this book for someone who is completely new.  Instead, find a book that provides practical, useful examples that you can put into action that immediately demonstrates core concepts and leave this book for your reference later on.  You *will* find this book useful as you get more experience but learning will be a painful process if you rely on this book to learn from.

B005DKLPQ0|5.0|If you are looking at this collection, then I'll assume you already know the music.  The Smiths were one of the most important and original bands of the 80s, and it's about time all this great music was re-released.  They were immensely popular in England, and while they are known in the US, they never reached the level of success here that they deserved.  I guess that's hardly surprising.  This is a really nice collection that will replace my original CDs.  Does it have everything?  Probably not, they released a lot of stuff.  However, it's got most of what you need.  So, I'll limit this review to the remasters themselves.  I haven't gotten through all the CDs in this box yet, but those that I have listened to sound excellent.  Johnny Marr supervised the remastering, and did a great job.  These CDs are not overly loud or brick-walled like some newer discs.  The levels are a bit higher than the originals, but in a good way.  If there is any difference in the applied compression then it was done correctly and musically.  I had the original vinyl and the original CD versions of most of these albums, and IMO the remasters here offer a significant improvement in sound quality across the board, especially on a good system.    I also think the packaging is clever and effective.  Each CD is contained in a miniature version of the original cardboard outer album sleeve, accurate right down to the stickers on the front and free poster included with \\"Rank\\".  The cool thing is that the inside album sleeves are also included.  So, the CD is inside a more slippery inner paper sleeve printed like the original, and then that fits in the outer cardboard sleeve, just like a vinyl album.  I hate CDs that come in just a cardboard sleeve because they are difficult to get in and out without getting fingerprints all over them, and I generally have to put them in a separate jewel case.  However, the inner paper album sleeves that come with these are very easy to get the CD in and out of, and then these slide right into the cardboard outer sleeve.  So, the packaging is fun, compact, and easy to use, without the constant fuss to avoid smudges like normal cardboard sleeves.   So, overall this is a must-have for the Smith's fan, even if you have the original CDs.  The price is pretty low for all that you get, and the remastered versions sound great.  Don't wait too long - good stuff like this seems to disappear way too quickly these days to make room for more awful, syntho-dance crap.

B0002858YS|4.0|I mainly listen to older alternative and post-punk music from the 70s and 80s, and I am not impressed by that many newer bands.  However, the Killers definitely impress me.  This is a great album that stands up to many of the classics from that earlier period, but I wouldn't call it retro.  They are clearly influenced by the greats from the previous generation, but they still have a modern sound, and more importantly, have their own sound.  They are hard-edged, but still have great hooks and melodies, like the Jam or old U2 (not that they sound like either one).  As great as the music is on the CD, the recording and production quality is so bad that it's actually distracting from the music IMO.  The first three tracks are almost painful to listen to a on decent system, and the rest are not much better.  Everything is distorted - the vocals are distorted, the bass is distorted, the drums are distorted, etc.  Either this was recorded on a 25-year-old cassette boom-box, or the engineer is deaf.  Unfortunately, it's probably the work of another misguided producer trying to make it sound more \\"analog\\", in which case it's clear that no one involved has ever actually heard a decent analog recording.  Contrary to current popular belief, analog does not equal distortion!    I suppose if most people just listen to this on their iPod cranked-up to the point where it's distorting anyway, maybe they don't notice.  IMO though, the bad recording is not doing the great music here any justice, and is actually detracting from it.  Keep in mind, I listen to old, low-budget alternative and punk recordings from 30+ years ago, so I'm not being a picky audiophile looking for perfection here!  However, there's really no reason for a major 2005 release to sound like something recorded on a 4-track in someone's garage in 1977.  So, to sum up:  A definite 5-stars for the music, but -1 stars for the really terrible production.  If the overly distorted sound was a creative choice, it was a badone.  I'm looking to buy a Killers live CD in the hopes that these songs are better to listen to on it.
030788743X|5.0|I caught a review of this book in Entertainment Weekly, and was immediately intrigued by the excerpts that seemed to dig from the culture of my childhood, such as Superfriends and Back to the Future (I was born in the 80s). Typically I feel that EW's reviews aren't that well researched, credible, or otherwise useful for anything other than a laugh, but that's a story for another day. Long story short, they were right on this one.  Backstory (Spoiler free!): Basically, it's a not too distant future, where the world is ravaged by a myriad of man-made problems, and humanity is dying off slowly. Since the world sucks so bad, they spend almost all their free time in a Virtual Reality called the OASIS. It has everything you could possibly want, split into a literal virtual universe: entertainment, education, social settings, even planets with the OASIS that are just recreations of a person's hometown that they can hang out in if they want to live in nostalgia.  The creator of the OASIS, and the richest man on Earth, James Halliday, dies. On the day of his death, he releases a video discussing the first \\"easter egg\\" ever found in a game, and how he planted one of his own inside his greatest game... the OASIS itself. Whoever finds this egg in his game... inherits his entire fortune, and the entire OASIS. Needless to say, the search for his easter egg quickly becomes the biggest contest in the history of mankind.  The story centers around a teenage kid in Oklahoma, Wade Watts, and his quest to find this easter egg. Along the way, he makes friends, and tons of enemies all looking to score the egg first.  The real brilliance of this book, I think, and its true calling card, is not only how it's chock full of geeky references, mostly from the 80s (but there's plenty of more modern stuff thrown in too), but how brilliantly the justification for all the references to Heathers, Family Ties, Ally Sheedy, Peter Gabriel, etc are woven into the story. Basically, James Halliday was obsessed with the era in which he grew up as a kid (the 80s, of course). So naturally, when he dies and promises all his fortune to anyone who can figure out his game within a game, it becomes everyone in the world's business to learn and appreciate the pop culture that he loved so much, if only to try and figure out clues where his easter egg is hidden. So you have people referencing and obsessing over movies, TV shows, and music from the 80s, when the real world setting is somewhere in the 2050s.  And it works. Seamlessly. It's fantastic. It gives the author a way to regale us with his amazing knowledge of geek culture, without making the story feel like an afterthought. And trust me, there's plenty to like in the actual story too. There's plenty of action (both of the virtual and real world variety), and even a bit of a heist in the middle that was well crafted. I was riveted the whole time, and honestly, it felt a lot shorter than the 350ish pages it weighs in at. Which is a good thing. Though for some reason, I had the voice of Curtis Armstrong (Booger from Revenge of the Nerds, and a billion other movies and shows) in my head for the protagonist. Not exactly sure why, but I'm sure the author wouldn't mind that comparison.  But seriously. It's some seriously nifty sci fi, and my favorite book this year so far, just for the amazing amounts of originality found within. Your results may vary, of course, depending on which decade you hold fond memories of.

B005Q1W10A|4.0|Considered one of the most successful \\"darlings\\" in the history of television, the first season of Downton Abbey achieved international success and a worldwide following. Its second season includes the same likable (and some not so likable) characters, but in an entirely new world...  When last we left Downton, the house was in turmoil at the recent news that war has begun with Germany. Some time later, the inhabitants of the great old estate have seen their lives immensely changed. Robert (Hugh Bonneville) feels inadequate and wants greatly to once more go to battle rather than staying at home and raising the morale of the women, and his wife Cora (Elizabeth McGovern) is too distracted to take much notice of his melancholy mood. Each of his daughters have embarked on individual quests to be of some use: Mary (Michelle Dockery) is both pining and praying for her beloved Matthew (Dan Stevens) at the front, Edith (Laura Carmichael) is learning to drive a tractor (among other things), and Sybil (Jessica Brown Findlay) is hoping to become a nurse at the local hospital.  Downstairs, Carson (Jim Carter) is attempting to contend with a limited staff and even scarcer resources. Mrs. Hughes (Phyllis Logan) is confronted with the antics and perils of a brand new maid with ideas above her station, and the would-be-romance between Anna (Joan Froggatt) and Bates (Brendan Coyle) is put on temporary hold when his wife (Maria Doyle Kennedy) arrives with a startling proposition.  While each of the characters confronts their fears and struggles to find a way to fit into a world that is altering all around them, the second season powers forward with purpose and sentimentality... although it does sometimes stray into convenient clich√©s and predictable outcomes. One of the more remarkable things about it is how this series manages to involve us so completely in the lives of its many protagonists, whether we love or hate them, find them irritating or are frustrated with their bad choices. This season finds a dramatic change in Lady Mary for the better, makes us feel sorry for Thomas (I never thought it possible!), and invokes tears of both joy and sorrow as each installment comes to a close. The writing is sometimes magnificent and sometimes subpar, its main problem in moving too quickly through situations (in some instances, I would have advocated slowing down) and not permitting the audience to really spend much time on some of the romantic relationships. It also on occasion suffers from \\"telling\\" rather than \\"showing,\\" which is a shame. Some moments are very, very good (such as an attempted assassination, the return of a missing soldier, and the final ten minutes of the finale) while others are downright cringe-worthy - Mrs. Bates in particular is too conveniently evil for my taste.     Season two has taken a lot of heat from critics and audiences alike for its predictable storylines and rampant clich√©s. Much of this criticism is deserved, since while some of the character development is believable (such as the transformations of Mary and Edith) other departures are completely unexpected and unlikely. The series hits all the right emotional notes but doesn't quite have the spark of its predecessor. Gone are all the wonderful conversations and moments that were used to build character and humor and forthright plot-driven exposition is in its place. Even so, there are some terrific one-liners from Maggie Smith and the happy ending to one couple's crisis is more than enough to redeem any of its mistakes.

0812992792|5.0|Adam Johnson writes with authority about the essentially unknown North Korean culture and civilization. Kim Jong il's force-fed propaganda controls the people so consummately that their identities are squeezed from their minds and replaced with a state-sponsored life and perspective. The life of a North Korean is not the pursuit of happiness or self-actualization. It is solely to survive, like an insect or a rodent. To live, you must become a shell, an unquestionably loyal nationalist.<br /><br />What Johnson realizes so well in his debut novel are the conflicts, confrontations, and abysses between the self that has been annihilated and the social structure that replaces the self. Every word you utter is weighed, and could be twisted as subversive. You are subjected to daily propaganda reports through loudspeakers connected to your home. People are traumatized from the cradle to the grave, and your individual thoughts are a threat to your security and safety. You are raised to be a complete subject of the state, and to wear the skin of trauma that is inflicted daily.<br /><br />Jun Do is a survivor of famine and abandonment. His father ran an orphanage, and Jun Do was expected to impersonate an orphan from an early age. His strength, talents, and stamina lead him along an epic path. From his high seas and espionage adventures on a fishing vessel, where he develops his first chosen father-son emotional relationship, to the deprivations and torture of the prison mines, to the corrupt corridors of power, where his skill of impersonation becomes his sword and precarious shield, Jun Do's life literally morphs into a fabled one.  He learns to act alone, and yet to connect with the hearts of others.<br /><br />\\"Today, tomorrow...A day is nothing. A day is just a match you strike after the ten thousand matches before it have gone out,\\" says the tragic, beautifully wounded actress, Sun Moon, who has made persona an art, and who once captured the hearts of all the citizens, including her husband, Commander Ga. Jun Do's transformations, internal and external, bring him squarely into the receptacles of Commander Ga, Sun Moon, and the \\"Dear Leader\\" himself.<br /><br />This postmodern novel is told via stunning juxtapositions, between the controllers and subjects of a treacherous society and the inner will of the individual. The historical context is authentic, complex, layered, and detailed. Chapters alternate between Jun Do and a nameless interrogator, which progress to an operatic denouement. This isn't the kind of novel that grabs you immediately; there are many ambiguities and inchoate events that build gradually, stone by stone, erecting an explosive story that tunnels through the doom of a raw reality, to a bloodletting dystopian myth, and into the chambers of a sequestered heart.ncluding her husband, Commander Ga. B005K8QIS0|5.0|Case Histories, a British mini-series, just wrapped up on \\"Masterpiece Mystery\\" on television.  This new adaptation of Kate Atkinson's work, starring Jason Isaacs as private detective Jackson Brodie, is excellent.  I've read all four of Atkinson's Brodie novels and really enjoyed them, so I was fairly excited to see this program and wondered if they'd do a good job.  Didn't have to worry.<br /><br />As one reviewer noted, any TV show that has the smarts to cast the underrated and underused Isaacs is worth checking out; this one also has good writing and great casting of all the other actors going for it too.  I thought the writers did an excellent job of paring down Atkinson's novels for television - cutting out unnecessary plot lines and characters to streamline the story and make it work quite well, at least for me.  I watched the show with my husband, who hasn't read the books, and he felt everything made sense and really enjoyed the program.  I remember while I read the books that there were parts I felt were unneeded, which are the same parts the writers took out.<br /><br />The main thrust of the story is that Brodie, ex-military and police officer, now private detective, gets involved in various investigations, some of which are connected.  Each episode has 3 cases in it.  As some reviewers have commented, there is a certain amount of coincidence in the cases being connected, but this may not bother some people (it doesn't bother me that much).  And some of the changes in the plot lines from book to TV show were necessary for clarity and time concerns, so an incident that seems more random in the book is a bit manipulated on TV.  There are also a couple of major plot lines that are completely missing from the TV version, but their exclusion is necessary to create a simpler and clearer story.<br /><br />What's great about the series is the acting - from the main characters to the minor ones.  Brodie's assistant Deborah and his daughter Marlee are especially memorable, as is Reggie from the third installment.  Isaacs is perfect as Brodie, capturing both the hard-boiled detective and the loving father with equal ease.  It's nice to see him playing a real person, rather than a plot-catalyst bad guy or bad-guy sidekick.<br /><br />Highly recommended to anyone who enjoys quality drama for grownups; be aware there's some cussing and a little sex here and there.morable, as is Reggie from the third installment.
